<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Continent</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Continent_(disambiguation)" title="Continent (disambiguation)">Continent (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Continental_models-Australia.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="755" data-file-width="1488" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Continental_models-Australia.gif/300px-Continental_models-Australia.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Continental_models-Australia.gif/450px-Continental_models-Australia.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Continental_models-Australia.gif/600px-Continental_models-Australia.gif 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Continental_models-Australia.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Animated, colour-coded map showing the various continents and regions. <a href="/wiki/Boundaries_between_the_continents_of_Earth" title="Boundaries between the continents of Earth">Depending on the convention and model</a>, some continents may be consolidated or subdivided: for example, <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> is most often subdivided into <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> (red shades), while <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> are sometimes recognised as <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">one American continent</a> (green shades).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <b>continent</b> is one of several very large <a href="/wiki/Land#Land_mass" title="Land">landmasses</a> of the <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">world</a>. Generally identified by <a href="/wiki/Convention_(norm)" title="Convention (norm)">convention</a> rather than any strict criteria, up to seven regions are commonly regarded as continents. Ordered from largest in size to smallest, they are: <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatlGeo1_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeo1-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">Geologically</a> the continents largely correspond to areas of <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">continental crust</a> that are found on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_plate" title="Continental plate">continental plates</a>. However some areas of continental crust are <a href="/wiki/Submerged_continent" title="Submerged continent">regions covered with water</a> not usually included in the list of continents. The area referred to as <a href="/wiki/Zealandia" title="Zealandia">Zealandia</a> is one such area (see <a href="/wiki/Continent#Submerged_continents" title="Continent">submerged continents</a> below).</p>
<p>Islands are frequently grouped with a neighbouring continent to divide all the world's land into geopolitical regions. Under this scheme, most of the island countries and territories in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> are grouped together with the <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">continent of Australia</a> to form a geopolitical region called <i><a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a></i>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_and_application">Definitions and application</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dymaxion_map_unfolded-no-ocean.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="638" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Dymaxion_map_unfolded-no-ocean.png/220px-Dymaxion_map_unfolded-no-ocean.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Dymaxion_map_unfolded-no-ocean.png/330px-Dymaxion_map_unfolded-no-ocean.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Dymaxion_map_unfolded-no-ocean.png/440px-Dymaxion_map_unfolded-no-ocean.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dymaxion_map_unfolded-no-ocean.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Dymaxion_map" title="Dymaxion map">Dymaxion map</a> shows land masses with minimal shape distortion.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By convention, "continents are understood to be large, continuous, discrete masses of land, ideally separated by expanses of water."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis_.26_Wigen.2C_The_Myth_of_Continents199721_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis_.26_Wigen.2C_The_Myth_of_Continents199721-2">[2]</a></sup> Several of the seven conventionally recognized continents are not discrete landmasses separated completely by water. The criterion "large" leads to arbitrary classification: <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, with a surface area of 2,166,086 square kilometres (836,330 sq mi) is considered the world's largest island, while Australia, at 7,617,930 square kilometres (2,941,300 sq mi) is deemed the smallest continent.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s major landmasses all have coasts on a single, continuous <a href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">World Ocean</a>, which is divided into a number of principal <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceanic</a> components by the continents and various geographic criteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNAoO_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNAoO-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent">Extent</span></h3>
<p>The most restricted meaning of <i>continent</i> is that of a continuous<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> area of land or mainland, with the coastline and any land boundaries forming the edge of the continent. In this sense the term <i><a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a></i> (sometimes referred to in Britain as "the Continent") is used to refer to mainland Europe, excluding <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">islands</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, and the term <i>continent of Australia</i> may refer to the <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">mainland of Australia</a>, excluding <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>. Similarly, the <i><a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">continental United States</a></i> refers to the 48 contiguous states and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a> in central North America and may include <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> in the northwest of the continent (the two being separated by <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>), while excluding <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a> in the oceans.</p>
<p>From the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> or <a href="/wiki/Physical_geography" title="Physical geography">physical geography</a>, <i>continent</i> may be extended beyond the confines of continuous dry land to include the shallow, submerged adjacent area (the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">islands</a> on the shelf (<a href="/wiki/Island#Continental_islands" title="Island">continental islands</a>), as they are structurally part of the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>From this perspective the edge of the continental shelf is the true edge of the continent, as shorelines vary with changes in sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ollier_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ollier-8">[8]</a></sup> In this sense the islands of Great Britain and Ireland are part of Europe, while Australia and the island of New Guinea together form a continent.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Island_nations.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="863" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Island_nations.svg/300px-Island_nations.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Island_nations.svg/450px-Island_nations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Island_nations.svg/600px-Island_nations.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Island_nations.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of <a href="/wiki/Island_country" title="Island country">island countries</a>: these states are often grouped geographically with a neighboring continental landmass.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As a <a href="/wiki/Social_constructionism" title="Social constructionism">cultural construct</a>, the concept of a continent may go beyond the continental shelf to include <a href="/wiki/Island#Oceanic_islands" title="Island">oceanic islands</a> and continental fragments. In this way, Iceland is considered part of Europe and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> part of Africa. Extrapolating the concept to its extreme, some geographers group the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Plate" title="Australian Plate">Australian continental plate</a> with other islands in the Pacific into one continent called <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>. This divides the entire land surface of Earth into continents or quasi-continents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Separation">Separation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boundaries_between_the_continents_of_Earth" title="Boundaries between the continents of Earth">Boundaries between the continents of Earth</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_transcontinental_countries" title="List of transcontinental countries">List of transcontinental countries</a></div>
<p>The ideal criterion that each continent be a discrete landmass is commonly relaxed due to historical conventions. Of the seven most globally recognized continents, only Antarctica and Australia are completely separated from other continents by ocean. Several continents are defined not as absolutely distinct bodies but as "<i>more or less</i> discrete masses of land".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis_.26_Wigen.2C_The_Myth_of_Continents199735_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis_.26_Wigen.2C_The_Myth_of_Continents199735-10">[10]</a></sup> Asia and Africa are joined by the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Suez" title="Isthmus of Suez">Isthmus of Suez</a>, and North and South America by the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">Isthmus of Panama</a>. In both cases, there is no complete separation of these landmasses by water (disregarding the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>, which are both narrow and shallow, as well as being artificial). Both these <a href="/wiki/Isthmus" title="Isthmus">isthmuses</a> are very narrow compared to the bulk off the landmasses they unite.</p>
<p>North America and South America are treated as separate continents in the seven-continent model. However, they may also be viewed as a single continent known as America or the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>. This viewpoint was common in the United States until World War II, and remains prevalent in some Asian six-continent models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> This remains the more common vision in Latin American countries, Spain, Portugal, France, Italy and Greece, where they are taught as a single continent.</p>
<p>The criterion of a discrete landmass is completely disregarded if the continuous landmass of Eurasia is classified as two separate continents: Europe and Asia. Physiographically, Europe and South Asia are peninsulas of the Eurasian landmass. However, Europe is widely considered a continent with its comparatively large land area of 10,180,000 square kilometres (3,930,000 sq mi), while South Asia, with less than half that area, is considered a subcontinent. The alternative view—in geology and geography—that Eurasia is a single continent results in a six-continent view of the world. Some view separation of Eurasia into Asia and Europe as a residue of <a href="/wiki/Eurocentrism" title="Eurocentrism">Eurocentrism</a>: "In physical, cultural and historical diversity, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> are comparable to the entire European landmass, not to a single European country. [...]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis_.26_Wigen.2C_The_Myth_of_Continents1997.3F_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis_.26_Wigen.2C_The_Myth_of_Continents1997.3F-12">[12]</a></sup> However, for historical and cultural reasons,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2017)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> the view of Europe as a separate continent continues in several categorizations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>If continents are defined strictly as discrete landmasses, embracing all the contiguous land of a body, then Africa, Asia, and Europe form a single continent which may be referred to as <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a>. This produces a four-continent model consisting of Afro-Eurasia, America, Antarctica and Australia.</p>
<p>When sea levels were lower during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice ages</a>, greater areas of continental shelf were exposed as dry land, forming <a href="/wiki/Land_bridge" title="Land bridge">land bridges</a>. At those times <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia–New Guinea</a> was a single, continuous continent. Likewise the Americas and Afro-Eurasia were joined by the <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Bering land bridge</a>. Other islands such as Great Britain were joined to the mainlands of their continents. At that time there were just three discrete continents: Afro-Eurasia-America, Antarctica, and Australia-New Guinea.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Number">Number</span></h3>
<p><span id="Number"></span> There are several ways of distinguishing the continents:</p>

<td><b>Seven continents</b><br/>

<td><small><span style="background:#fed52e; border:0px solid #000; vertical-align:center; padding-right:1em; padding-top:0ex; padding-bottom:0ex; line-height:100%"> </span></small> Africa</td>

<td><small><span style="background:#0c0; border:0px solid #000; vertical-align:center; padding-right:1em; padding-top:0ex; padding-bottom:0ex; line-height:100%"> </span></small> North America</td>

<td><small><span style="background:#0040ff; border:0px solid #000; vertical-align:center; padding-right:1em; padding-top:0ex; padding-bottom:0ex; line-height:100%"> </span></small> Antarctica</td>
<td><small><span style="background:#c04080; border:0px solid #000; vertical-align:center; padding-right:1em; padding-top:0ex; padding-bottom:0ex; line-height:100%"> </span></small> Australia/Oceania</td>
</tr>
</table>
<ul>
<li>The seven-continent model is usually taught in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, parts of <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> and most <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_English-speaking_population" title="List of countries by English-speaking population">English-speaking countries</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></li>
<li>The six-continent combined-Eurasia model is mostly used in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.</li>
<li>The six-continent combined-America model is often used in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and its <a href="/wiki/List_of_French_possessions_and_colonies" title="List of French possessions and colonies">former possessions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAE_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-RAE-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAE_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-RAE-24">[24]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HEL_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-HEL-14">[14]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li>A five-continent model is obtained from this model by excluding Antarctica as uninhabited. This is used, for example in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Charter" title="Olympic Charter">Olympic Charter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OC_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-OC-26">[26]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The term <i><a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a></i> refers to a group of <a href="/wiki/Island_country" title="Island country">island countries</a> and territories in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, together with the <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">continent of Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HEL_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-HEL-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AoCA_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-AoCA-18">[18]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_islands" title="Pacific islands">Pacific islands</a> with ties to other continents (such as <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> or <a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">Easter Island</a>) are usually grouped with those continents rather than Oceania. This term is used in several different continental models instead of <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_divisions">Other divisions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercontinents">Supercontinents</span></h3>
<p>Aside from the conventionally known continents, the scope and meaning of the term <i>continent</i> varies. <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">Supercontinents</a>, largely in evidence earlier in the geological record, are landmasses that comprise more than one <a href="/wiki/Craton" title="Craton">craton</a> or continental core. These have included <a href="/wiki/Laurasia" title="Laurasia">Laurasia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaalbara" title="Vaalbara">Vaalbara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenorland" title="Kenorland">Kenorland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(supercontinent)" title="Columbia (supercontinent)">Columbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rodinia" title="Rodinia">Rodinia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subcontinents">Subcontinents</span></h3>
<p>Certain parts of continents are recognized as subcontinents, especially the large peninsulas separated from the main continental landmass by geographical features. The most notable examples are the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baldwin_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-27">[27]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_cone" title="Southern cone">southern cone</a> of South America and <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaskan peninsula</a> of North America are other examples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baldwin_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>In many of these cases, the "subcontinents" concerned are on different <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">tectonic plates</a> from the rest of the continent, providing a geological justification for the terminology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Molnar2015_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Molnar2015-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, generally reckoned as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_by_area" title="List of islands by area">world's largest island</a> on the northeastern periphery of the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American Plate</a>, is sometimes referred to as a subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FarmerCook2013_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FarmerCook2013-30">[30]</a></sup> This is a significant departure from the more conventional view of a subcontinent as comprising a very large peninsula on the fringe of a continent.</p>
<p>Where the Americas are viewed as a single continent (America), it is divided into two subcontinents (<a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallay2015_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gallay2015-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InnesHaron2000_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-InnesHaron2000-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vivares2014_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vivares2014-33">[33]</a></sup> or three (with <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a> being the third).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard2005_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leonard2005-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> When Eurasia is regarded as a single continent, Europe is treated as a subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baldwin_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Submerged_continents">Submerged continents</span></h3>
<p>Some areas of <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">continental crust</a> are largely covered by the sea and may be considered <a href="/wiki/Submerged_continent" title="Submerged continent">submerged continents</a>. Notable examples are <a href="/wiki/Zealandia" title="Zealandia">Zealandia</a>, emerging from the sea primarily in <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>, and the almost completely submerged <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Plateau" title="Kerguelen Plateau">Kerguelen Plateau</a> in the southern <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microcontinents">Microcontinents</span></h3>
<p>Some islands lie on sections of continental crust that have rifted and drifted apart from a main continental landmass. While not considered continents because of their relatively small size, they may be considered <a href="/wiki/Continental_fragment" title="Continental fragment">microcontinents</a>. <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, the largest example, is usually considered an island of Africa but has been referred to as "the eighth continent" from a <span class="cleanup-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">biological perspective</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Botanical_continents">Botanical continents</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WGSRPD_World.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1474" data-file-width="2693" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/WGSRPD_World.svg/300px-WGSRPD_World.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/WGSRPD_World.svg/450px-WGSRPD_World.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/WGSRPD_World.svg/600px-WGSRPD_World.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WGSRPD_World.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Botanical continents" defined by the <a href="/wiki/World_Geographical_Scheme_for_Recording_Plant_Distributions" title="World Geographical Scheme for Recording Plant Distributions">World Geographical Scheme for Recording Plant Distributions</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>"Continents" may be defined differently for specific purposes. The <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_Information_Standards" title="Biodiversity Information Standards">Biodiversity Information Standards</a> organization has developed the <a href="/wiki/World_Geographical_Scheme_for_Recording_Plant_Distributions" title="World Geographical Scheme for Recording Plant Distributions">World Geographical Scheme for Recording Plant Distributions</a>, used in many international plant databases. This scheme divides the world into nine "botanical continents". Some match the traditional geographical continents, but some differ significantly. Thus the Americas are divided between Northern America (Mexico northwards) and Southern America (Central America and the Caribbean southwards) rather than between North America and South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_continental_configurations">History of continental configurations</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_the_Earth" title="Geological history of the Earth">Geological history of the Earth</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_concept">History of the concept</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:189px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Strabo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1056" data-file-width="873" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Strabo.jpg/187px-Strabo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Strabo.jpg/281px-Strabo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Strabo.jpg/374px-Strabo.jpg 2x" width="187"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Strabo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Ancient Greek geographer <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> holding a globe showing <i>Europa</i> and <i>Asia</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_concepts_of_the_Old_World_continents">Early concepts of the Old World continents</span></h3>
<p>The term "continent" translates Greek <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">ἤπειρος</span>, properly "landmass, terra firma", the proper name of <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a> and later especially used of Asia (i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The first distinction between continents was made by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greek</a> mariners who gave the names Europe and Asia to the lands on either side of the waterways of the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a> strait, the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Marmara" title="Sea of Marmara">Sea of Marmara</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a> strait and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toynbee_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Toynbee-39">[39]</a></sup> The names were first applied just to lands near the coast and only later extended to include the hinterlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> But the division was only carried through to the end of navigable waterways and "... beyond that point the Hellenic geographers never succeeded in laying their finger on any inland feature in the physical landscape that could offer any convincing line for partitioning an indivisible Eurasia ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toynbee_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Toynbee-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ancient Greek thinkers subsequently debated whether Africa (then called <i>Libya</i>) should be considered part of Asia or a third part of the world. Division into three parts eventually came to predominate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> From the Greek viewpoint, the Aegean Sea was the center of the world; Asia lay to the east, Europe to the north and west, and Africa to the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis_.26_Wigen.2C_The_Myth_of_Continents199721.E2.80.9322_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis_.26_Wigen.2C_The_Myth_of_Continents199721.E2.80.9322-42">[42]</a></sup> The boundaries between the continents were not fixed. Early on, the Europe–Asia boundary was taken to run from the Black Sea along the <a href="/wiki/Rioni_River" title="Rioni River">Rioni River</a> (known then as the <i>Phasis</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>. Later it was viewed as running from the Black Sea through <a href="/wiki/Kerch_Strait" title="Kerch Strait">Kerch Strait</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Azov" title="Sea of Azov">Sea of Azov</a> and along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_River,_Russia" title="Don River, Russia">Don River</a> (known then as the <i>Tanais</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The boundary between Asia and Africa was generally taken to be the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> River. <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> in the 5th century BC, however, objected to the unity of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> being split into Asia and Africa ("Libya") and took the boundary to lie along the western border of Egypt, regarding Egypt as part of Asia. He also questioned the division into three of what is really a single landmass,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> a debate that continues nearly two and a half millennia later.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a>, in the 3rd century BC, noted that some geographers divided the continents by rivers (the Nile and the Don), thus considering them "islands". Others divided the continents by <a href="/wiki/Isthmus" title="Isthmus">isthmuses</a>, calling the continents "peninsulas". These latter geographers set the border between Europe and Asia at the isthmus between the Black Sea and the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>, and the border between Asia and Africa at the isthmus between the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and the mouth of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Bardawil" title="Lake Bardawil">Lake Bardawil</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="864" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg/220px-T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg/330px-T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg/440px-T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Medieval <a href="/wiki/T_and_O_map" title="T and O map">T and O map</a> showing the three continents as domains of the sons of <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a>—Asia to Sem (<a href="/wiki/Shem" title="Shem">Shem</a>), Europe to Iafeth (<a href="/wiki/Japheth" title="Japheth">Japheth</a>), and Africa to Cham (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ham,_son_of_Noah" title="Ham, son of Noah">Ham</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Through the Roman period and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, a few writers took the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Suez" title="Isthmus of Suez">Isthmus of Suez</a> as the boundary between Asia and Africa, but most writers continued to consider it the Nile or the western border of Egypt (Gibbon)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. In the Middle Ages, the world was usually portrayed on <a href="/wiki/T_and_O_map" title="T and O map">T and O maps</a>, with the T representing the waters dividing the three continents. By the middle of the 18th century, "the fashion of dividing Asia and Africa at the Nile, or at the Great Catabathmus [the boundary between Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>] farther west, had even then scarcely passed away".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_arrival_in_the_Americas">European arrival in the Americas</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> sailed across the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">West Indies</a> in 1492, sparking a period of European exploration of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>. But despite four voyages to the Americas, Columbus never believed he had reached a new continent—he always thought it was part of Asia.</p>
<p>In 1501, <a href="/wiki/Amerigo_Vespucci" title="Amerigo Vespucci">Amerigo Vespucci</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gon%C3%A7alo_Coelho" title="Gonçalo Coelho">Gonçalo Coelho</a> attempted to sail around what they considered the southern end of the Asian mainland into the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, passing through <a href="/wiki/Fernando_de_Noronha" title="Fernando de Noronha">Fernando de Noronha</a>. After reaching the coast of <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, they sailed a long way further south along the coast of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, confirming that this was a land of continental proportions and that it also extended much further south than Asia was known to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OGorman_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-OGorman-48">[48]</a></sup> On return to Europe, an account of the voyage, called <i>Mundus Novus</i> ("New World"), was published under Vespucci's name in 1502 or 1503,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-formisano1_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-formisano1-49">[49]</a></sup> although it seems that it had additions or alterations by another writer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zerubavel1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zerubavel1-50">[50]</a></sup> Regardless of who penned the words, <i>Mundus Novus</i> credited Vespucci with saying, "I have discovered a continent in those southern regions that is inhabited by more numerous people and animals than our Europe, or Asia or Africa",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-formisano2_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-formisano2-51">[51]</a></sup> the first known explicit identification of part of the Americas as a continent like the other three.</p>
<p>Within a few years the name "New World" began appearing as a name for South America on world maps, such as the Oliveriana (Pesaro) map of around 1504–1505. Maps of this time though, still showed <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> connected to Asia and showed South America as a separate land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zerubavel1_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zerubavel1-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:377px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7590" data-file-width="13708" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg/375px-Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg/563px-Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg/750px-Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg 2x" width="375"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Universalis Cosmographia</i>, Waldseemüller's 1507 world map—the first to show the Americas separate from Asia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1507 <a href="/wiki/Martin_Waldseem%C3%BCller" title="Martin Waldseemüller">Martin Waldseemüller</a> published a world map, <i><a href="/wiki/Waldseem%C3%BCller_map" title="Waldseemüller map">Universalis Cosmographia</a></i>, which was the first to show North and South America as separate from Asia and surrounded by water. A small inset map above the main map explicitly showed for the first time the Americas being east of Asia and separated from Asia by an ocean, as opposed to just placing the Americas on the left end of the map and Asia on the right end. In the accompanying book <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmographiae_Introductio" title="Cosmographiae Introductio">Cosmographiae Introductio</a></i>, Waldseemüller noted that the earth is divided into four parts, Europe, Asia, Africa and the fourth part, which he named "America" after Amerigo Vespucci's first name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zerubavel2_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zerubavel2-52">[52]</a></sup> On the map, the word "America" was placed on part of South America.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_word_continent">The word <i>continent</i></span></h3>
<p>From the 16th century the English noun <i>continent</i> was derived from the term <i>continent land</i>, meaning continuous or connected land<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-oed-53">[53]</a></sup> and translated from the Latin <i>terra continens</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The noun was used to mean "a connected or continuous tract of land" or <a href="/wiki/Mainland" title="Mainland">mainland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-oed-53">[53]</a></sup> It was not applied only to very large areas of land—in the 17th century, references were made to the <i>continents</i> (or mainlands) of <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and in 1745 to <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-oed-53">[53]</a></sup> The word <i>continent</i> was used in translating Greek and Latin writings about the three "parts" of the world, although in the original languages no word of exactly the same meaning as <i>continent</i> was used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis_.26_Wigen.2C_The_Myth_of_Continents199729_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis_.26_Wigen.2C_The_Myth_of_Continents199729-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>While <i>continent</i> was used on the one hand for relatively small areas of continuous land, on the other hand geographers again raised Herodotus's query about why a single large landmass should be divided into separate continents. In the mid-17th century, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Heylin" title="Peter Heylin">Peter Heylin</a> wrote in his <i>Cosmographie</i> that "A Continent is a great quantity of Land, not separated by any Sea from the rest of the World, as the whole Continent of Europe, Asia, Africa." In 1727, <a href="/wiki/Ephraim_Chambers" title="Ephraim Chambers">Ephraim Chambers</a> wrote in his <i>Cyclopædia,</i> "The world is ordinarily divided into two grand continents: the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">old</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">new</a>." And in his 1752 atlas, <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Bowen" title="Emanuel Bowen">Emanuel Bowen</a> defined a continent as "a large space of dry land comprehending many countries all joined together, without any separation by water. Thus Europe, Asia, and Africa is one great continent, as America is another."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> However, the old idea of Europe, Asia and Africa as "parts" of the world ultimately persisted with these being regarded as separate continents.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2310" data-file-width="2916" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png/250px-Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png/375px-Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png/500px-Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Hollandia Nova</i>, 1659 map prepared by <a href="/wiki/Joan_Blaeu" title="Joan Blaeu">Joan Blaeu</a> based on voyages by <a href="/wiki/Abel_Tasman" title="Abel Tasman">Abel Tasman</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Willem_Jansz" title="Willem Jansz">Willem Jansz</a>, this image shows a French edition of 1663</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beyond_four_continents">Beyond four continents</span></h3>
<p>From the late 18th century, some geographers started to regard North America and South America as two parts of the world, making five parts in total. Overall though, the fourfold division prevailed well into the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis30_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis30-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Europeans discovered <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a> in 1606, but for some time it was taken as part of Asia. By the late 18th century, some geographers considered it a continent in its own right, making it the sixth (or fifth for those still taking America as a single continent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis30_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-lewis30-57">[57]</a></sup> In 1813, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Butler_(schoolmaster)" title="Samuel Butler (schoolmaster)">Samuel Butler</a> wrote of Australia as "<a href="/wiki/New_Holland_(Australia)" title="New Holland (Australia)">New Holland</a>, an immense island, which some geographers dignify with the appellation of another continent" and the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> was just as equivocal some decades later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> was sighted in 1820 during the <a href="/wiki/First_Russian_Antarctic_Expedition" title="First Russian Antarctic Expedition">First Russian Antarctic Expedition</a> and described as a continent by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wilkes" title="Charles Wilkes">Charles Wilkes</a> on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Exploring_Expedition" title="United States Exploring Expedition">United States Exploring Expedition</a> in 1838, the last continent identified, although a great "Antarctic" (antipodean) landmass had been anticipated for millennia. An 1849 atlas labelled Antarctica as a continent but few atlases did so until after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the mid-19th century, atlases published in the United States more commonly treated North and South America as separate continents, while atlases published in Europe usually considered them one continent. However, it was still not uncommon for American atlases to treat them as one continent up until World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis32_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis32-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the 1950s, most U.S. geographers divided the Americas into two continents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis32_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-lewis32-60">[60]</a></sup> With the addition of Antarctica, this made the seven-continent model. However, this division of the Americas never appealed to <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin Americans</a>, who saw their region spanning an <span lang="es" xml:lang="es"><i>América</i></span> as a single landmass, and there the conception of six continents remains dominant, as it does in scattered other countries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Some geographers regard Europe and Asia together as a single continent, dubbed <i><a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In this model, the world is divided into six continents, with North America and South America considered separate continents.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(May 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">Continental crust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">Plate tectonics</a></div>
<p>Geologists use the term <i>continent</i> in a different manner from geographers, where a continent is defined by continental crust: a platform of <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rock</a>, largely of <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granitic</a> composition. Some geologists restrict the term 'continent' to portions of the crust built around stable <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> "shield", typically 1.5 to 3.8 billion years old, called a <a href="/wiki/Craton" title="Craton">craton</a>. The craton itself is an <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(geology)" title="Accretion (geology)">accretionary</a> complex of ancient mobile belts (mountain belts) from earlier cycles of <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Continental_collision" title="Continental collision">continental collision</a> and break-up from plate tectonic activity. An outward-thickening veneer of younger, minimally deformed <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a> covers much of the craton. The margins of geologic continents are characterized by currently active or relatively recently active mobile belts and deep troughs of accumulated marine or <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">deltaic</a> <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a>. Beyond the margin, there is either a <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a> and drop off to the <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basaltic</a> <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_basin" title="Oceanic basin">ocean basin</a> or the margin of another continent, depending on the current plate-tectonic setting of the continent. A continental boundary does not have to be a body of water. Over geologic time, continents are periodically submerged under large epicontinental seas, and continental collisions result in a continent becoming attached to another continent. The current geologic era is relatively anomalous in that so much of the continental areas are "high and dry"; that is, many parts of the continents that were once below sea level are now elevated well above it due to changes in sea levels and the subsequent uplifting of those continental areas from tectonic activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plates_tect2_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2844" data-file-width="4167" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plates_tect2_en.svg/350px-Plates_tect2_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plates_tect2_en.svg/525px-Plates_tect2_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plates_tect2_en.svg/700px-Plates_tect2_en.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plates_tect2_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The tectonic plates underlying the continents and oceans</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some argue that continents are accretionary crustal "rafts" that, unlike the denser basaltic crust of the ocean basins, are not subjected to destruction through the plate tectonic process of subduction. This accounts for the great age of the rocks comprising the continental cratons. By this definition, Eastern Europe, India and some other regions could be regarded as continental masses distinct from the rest of Eurasia because they have separate ancient shield areas (i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_European_craton" title="East European craton">East European craton</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_craton" title="Indian craton">Indian craton</a>). Younger mobile belts (such as the <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Ural Mountains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>) mark the boundaries between these regions and the rest of Eurasia.</p>
<p>There are many <a href="/wiki/Continental_fragment" title="Continental fragment">microcontinents, or continental fragments</a>, that are built of continental crust but do not contain a craton. Some of these are fragments of <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a> or other ancient cratonic continents: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zealandia_(continent)" title="Zealandia (continent)">Zealandia</a>, which includes <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_New_Zealand" title="Geography of New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_New_Caledonia" title="Geography of New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Madagascar" title="Geography of Madagascar">Madagascar</a>; the northern <a href="/wiki/Mascarene_Plateau" title="Mascarene Plateau">Mascarene Plateau</a>, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a>. Other islands, such as several in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a>, are composed largely of granitic rock as well, but all continents contain both granitic and basaltic crust, and there is no clear boundary as to which islands would be considered microcontinents under such a definition. The <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Plateau" title="Kerguelen Plateau">Kerguelen Plateau</a>, for example, is largely volcanic, but is associated with the breakup of Gondwanaland and is considered a microcontinent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> whereas volcanic <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Iceland" title="Geography of Iceland">Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Islands" title="Hawaiian Islands">Hawaii</a> are not. The <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a> are margins of the <a href="/wiki/Laurasia" title="Laurasia">Laurasian</a> continent—only separated by inland seas flooding its margins.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">Plate tectonics</a> offers yet another way of defining continents. Today, Europe and most of Asia constitute the unified <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian Plate</a>, which is approximately coincident with the geographic Eurasian continent excluding India, Arabia, and far eastern Russia. India contains a central shield, and the geologically recent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himalaya" title="Himalaya">Himalaya</a> mobile belt forms its northern margin. North America and South America are separate continents, the connecting <a href="/wiki/Isthmus" title="Isthmus">isthmus</a> being largely the result of <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanism</a> from relatively recent subduction tectonics. North American continental rocks extend to Greenland (a portion of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Shield" title="Canadian Shield">Canadian Shield</a>), and in terms of plate boundaries, the North American plate includes the easternmost portion of the Asian land mass. Geologists do not use these facts to suggest that eastern Asia is part of the North American continent, even though the plate boundary extends there; the word continent is usually used in its geographic sense and additional definitions ("continental rocks," "plate boundaries") are used as appropriate.</p>
<p>The movement of plates has caused the formation and break-up of continents over time, including occasional formation of a supercontinent that contains most or all of the continents. The supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(supercontinent)" title="Columbia (supercontinent)">Columbia</a> or Nuna formed during a period of 2.0–1.8 billion years and broke up about 1.5–1.3 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhao1_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhao1-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhao2_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhao2-66">[66]</a></sup> The supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Rodinia" title="Rodinia">Rodinia</a> is thought to have formed about 1 billion years ago and to have embodied most or all of Earth's continents, and broken up into eight continents around 600 million years ago. The eight continents later re-assembled into another supercontinent called <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>; Pangaea broke up into Laurasia (which became North America and Eurasia) and Gondwana (which became the remaining continents).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Highest_and_lowest_points">Highest and lowest points</span></h2>

<p>† The lowest exposed points are given for North America and Antarctica. The lowest non-submarine bedrock elevations in these continents are the trough beneath <a href="/wiki/Jakobshavn_Glacier" title="Jakobshavn Glacier">Jakobshavn Glacier</a> (−1,512 metres (−4,961 ft)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>) and <a href="/wiki/Bentley_Subglacial_Trench" title="Bentley Subglacial Trench">Bentley Subglacial Trench</a> (−2,540 metres (−8,330 ft)), but these are covered by kilometers of ice.</p>
<p>Some sources list the <a href="/wiki/Kuma%E2%80%93Manych_Depression" title="Kuma–Manych Depression">Kuma–Manych Depression</a> (a remnant of the <a href="/wiki/Paratethys" title="Paratethys">Paratethys</a>) as the geological border between Europe and Asia. This would place the Caucasus outside of Europe, thus making <a href="/wiki/Mont_Blanc" title="Mont Blanc">Mont Blanc</a> (elevation 4810 m) in the <a href="/wiki/Graian_Alps" title="Graian Alps">Graian Alps</a> the highest point in Europe - the lowest point would still be the shore of the Caspian Sea.</p>

<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Ibero-America, North America usually designates a region (<i>subcontinente</i> in Spanish) of the Americas containing Canada, the United States, and Mexico, and often Greenland, Saint Pierre and Miquelon, and Bermuda; the land bridge of Central America is generally considered a subregion of North America.<a class="external text" href="http://mx.encarta.msn.com/encyclopedia_761562468/Norteam%C3%A9rica.html" rel="nofollow">Norteamérica (Mexican version)</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090130000000/http://mx.encarta.msn.com/encyclopedia_761562468/Norteam%C3%A9rica.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 30 January 2009 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>./<a class="external text" href="http://es.encarta.msn.com/encyclopedia_761562468/Norteam%C3%A9rica.html" rel="nofollow">(Spaniard version)</a>. <i>Encarta Online Encyclopedia.</i>. <a class="external text" href="https://www.webcitation.org/5kwRLFsmt?url=http://es.encarta.msn.com/encyclopedia_761562468/Norteam%C3%A9rica.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2009-10-31.</span></li>
